\a al·ªãk
\ps n
\ge dish
\ru –ø–æ—Å—É–¥–∞
\nt Cincius writes this with e, i.e. alek, while Doerfer writes it with a
dot under the e. According to Burykin, the letter e after l stands

written here. Robbek writes it alik (i.e. without the long vowel).

\lx amar
\a ama
\ps nom
\ge last
\ge behind
\nt In February 2010 NA and BP decided to break down spatial and
temporal adverbs/relational nouns into their composite parts. For
a large part, this means defining a root which does not exist by
itself, but which can be identified in many case-marked forms.
One such root is amar 'behind'
\dt 28/Feb/2012

\lx amarg·ªãlta
\a amarg·ªãltan
\ps adv
\ge one.after.the.other
\ru –≥—É—Å—å–∫–æ–º, –≤–µ—Ä–µ–Ω–∏—Ü–µ–π, –ø–æ –æ—á–µ—Ä–µ–¥–∏, –¥—Ä—É–≥ –∑–∞ –¥—Ä—É–≥–æ–º
\nt Robbek writes this without the final -n; in 2007 I got several
instances of this word with the meaning of 'drug za drugom' in
elicitation sessions; in 2009, Evdokija Grigorevna used
'amalta:tkan', and when I asked Rimma Maksimovna, she said
'amargiltan' means 'zadnie', and also 'predki'

\lx erek
\a ere
\a er
\a e
\a u
\a oro
\ps pron
\ge prox
\nt Proximal demonstrative. The full form is used in the Nominative;
the form in ere is used in the Accusative (ere-w), Instrumental
(ere-ch), Allative-Locative (ere-kle), Allative-Prolative (ere-kli); the
form in er is used in the Comitative (er-n'un), Allative (er-teki),
Elative (er-gich), and Similative (er-gechin); the most reduced
form e is used in the Dative (e-du), Locative (e-le), Prolative (e-li),
and Ablative (e-duk). In February 2010 we decided that the spatial
adverbs eweski 'ahead, to here' and ewgile 'this side/closer' are
probably derived from erek - perhaps with some 'contamination'
from aw- 'where'? So we glossed them accordingly
\dt 03/Mar/2012
